<span class="italic"><a title="Link to journal home page" href="https://doi.org/10.1039/2040-3372/2009">Nanoscale</a></span>, 2018, <strong>10</strong>, 18786-18794</div><h1 id="sect207"><span class="title_heading">N-Rich carbon-coated Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> ultrafine nanocrystals derived from ZIF-67 as an advanced anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></h1><p class="header_text">
      <span class="bold">

      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Hongshuai&nbsp;Hou (orcid.org/0000-0001-8201-4614) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0001-8201-4614"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>*<span class="bold"> and </span>

            Xiaobo 
            Ji
          
        
      </span><span class="orcid"><a target="_blank" title="Select to open ORCID record for Xiaobo&nbsp;Ji (orcid.org/0000-0002-5405-7913) in a new window" id="connect-orcid-link" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-5405-7913"><img id="orcid-id-logo" src="/content/NewImages/orcid_16x16.png" alt="ORCID logo"></a></span>
      <br><span class="italic">College of Chemistry and Chemical Engineering, Central South University, Changsha, 410083, China. E-mail: <a href="mailto:hs-hou@csu.edu.cn">hs-hou@csu.edu.cn</a>;   Tel: +86 731-88877237</span>
    </p><div id="art-admin"><span class="italic bold">Received 
      13th July 2018
    </span><span class="bold italic">, Accepted 18th September 2018</span></div><p class="bold italic">First published on 19th September 2018</p><hr><div class="abstract"><h2>Abstract</h2><p>Transition metal sulfides (TMSs) have been extensively studied as electrode materials for sodium-ion batteries by virtue of their high theoretical capacity. However, the poor cyclability limits the practical application of TMSs in sodium ion batteries. In this study, N-rich carbon-coated Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> ultrafine nanocrystal (Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC) was prepared by utilizing ZIF-67 as a precursor through continuous carbonization and sulfuration processes, exhibiting ultrafine nanocrystals with a diameter of about 5 nm. When utilized as the anode for sodium ion batteries, the nanohybrid material exhibits remarkable cycling performance with a high specific capacity of 420.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at the current density of 100 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 100 cycles, indicating that the cycling performance is strengthened by the nitrogen-doped carbon coating. Impressively, the obtained material shows good rate performances with reversible specific capacities of 386.7, 284.0, and 151.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at 400, 1000, and 1400 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively, due to the high surface-capacitance contribution and porous structure inherited from the precursor, which finally results in the increase in infiltration of electrolyte and the accelerating diffusion rate of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>. This study sheds light on the routes to improve the performance of TMSs@nitrogen-doped carbon nanohybrid materials for sodium ion batteries.</p></div><hr>
    
      
      <h2 id="sect284"><span class="a_heading">1. Introduction</span></h2>
      <span>In recent years, transition metal sulfides (TMSs) have received considerable attention as an important family of functional materials in many applications, such as biomedicine,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit1"><sup><span class="sup_ref">1</span></sup></a> catalysis,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit2"><sup><span class="sup_ref">2</span></sup></a> and energy storage and conversion,<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit3"><sup><span class="sup_ref">3–5</span></sup></a> due to their special electronic structures and unique physical and chemical properties. In the field of electrochemical energy storage and conversion, lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been considered as the most practical energy-storage devices as a result of their high energy densities and stability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit6"><sup><span class="sup_ref">6,7</span></sup></a> However, the reserve of lithium in the earth crust is low and lithium recycling is not efficient enough, which would limit the development of the battery industry. Hence, devising an alternative is of great importance for future applications.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit8"><sup><span class="sup_ref">8–11</span></sup></a> Compared with that of lithium, sodium species possess the beneficial features of earth abundance, low cost and similar physical/chemical properties. Consequently, it is widely regarded that sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) can be a potential substitute for lithium-ion batteries in energy storage applications.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit12"><sup><span class="sup_ref">12,13</span></sup></a> Nevertheless, sodium (radius 1.02 Å, mass 22.99 g mol<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) possesses larger radius and heavier mass than lithium (radius 0.59 Å, mass 6.94 g mol<small><sup>−1</sup></small>), due to which the insertion/extraction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> into/out of the electrode is more difficult, resulting in low Na-storage capacity and poor cycling stability of batteries.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit14"><sup><span class="sup_ref">14–17</span></sup></a> Thus, it is necessary to excogitate a series of novel electrode materials with typical morphology, suitable size and relatively low cost to further enhance the Na-storage capacity, rate capability and cyclability of SIBs.</span>
      <p class="otherpara">As promising anode materials, transition metal sulfides can store Na<small><sup>+</sup></small><span class="italic">via</span> electrochemical multi-electron conversion mechanisms, showing high theoretical capacity.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit18"><sup><span class="sup_ref">18–20</span></sup></a> Moreover, the metal–sulfur bonds in metal sulfides are weaker than the metal–oxygen bonds in metal oxides, which are kinetically beneficial to accomplish conversion reactions.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit21"><sup><span class="sup_ref">21</span></sup></a> Unfortunately, some fatal problems such as large volume changes during the insertion/extraction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and poor electronic conductivity still exist in the conversion mechanisms and further result in capacity decay and unsatisfactory rate capability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit22"><sup><span class="sup_ref">22,23</span></sup></a> These abovementioned issues will impede the application of TMSs as anode materials for SIBs. In order to solve these problems, considerable efforts have been put forward. Among the reported studies, combining TMSs with carbon is an effective method.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19,24–28</span></sup></a> A hybrid SnS@graphene architecture has been prepared by Zhou <span class="italic">et al.</span> through combining SnS with graphene through a hydrothermal approach.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit19"><sup><span class="sup_ref">19</span></sup></a> The as-prepared SnS@graphene delivered an excellent specific capacity of 940 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 50 cycles at 200 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> when employed as a sodium-ion battery anode. Furthermore, polyaniline-coated TMSs have been applied to enhance cyclic stability.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit29"><sup><span class="sup_ref">29–31</span></sup></a> Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@polyaniline nanotubes were obtained by Zhou <span class="italic">et al.</span> using a self-templating route,<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> delivering a reversible capacity of 252.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 100 cycles at the current density of 200 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. Until now, some progress has been made in the research of TESs as SIBs electrode materials, whereas high capacity and excellent cycling stability have been difficult to achieve simultaneously.</p>
      <p class="otherpara">Metal–organic frameworks (MOFs), formed from metal ions and organic linkers, have received much attention as precursors to obtain nanostructured metal sulfide/carbon composites.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit32"><sup><span class="sup_ref">32–38</span></sup></a> Herein, N-rich carbon-coated Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> ultrafine nanocrystals derived from ZIF-67 display polyhedral structures with a surface area of 176.67 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and particle size of about 160 nm, and exist as ultrafine nanocrystals with a diameter of about 5 nm. The precursor ZIF-67, generated by linking cobalt ions and dimethyl imidazole, was carbonized in order to reduce Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small> to metal Co and transform organic units into inorganic carbon. Subsequently, Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC, with high surface area and mesoporous microstructure, was acquired by a solvothermal sulfuration reaction. Owing to the unique structural features and pore distribution, the obtained material can effectively remit the volume changes occurring during the insertion and extraction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and exhibit an excellent cyclability of 420.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> after 100 cycles at 100 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> as SIB anode materials.</p>
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect329"><span class="a_heading">2. Experimental section</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect332"><span class="b_heading">2.1 Materials synthesis</span></h3>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect335"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.1.1 Preparation of ZIF-67. </span>
          <span>All of the chemicals were directly used after purchase without further purification. The ZIF-67 precursors were obtained according to the reported literature.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit39"><sup><span class="sup_ref">39</span></sup></a> Typically, 0.45 g cobalt nitrate hexahydrate (Sinopharm Chemical) was first dissolved in 3 mL deionized (DI) water and then, the second solution was obtained by dissolving 5.5 g 2-methylimidazole (Hmim, Energy Chemical) in 20 mL DI water. After complete dissolution, the solution of 2-methylimidazole was poured into the former pink cobalt nitrate solution quickly, and the two solutions were mixed under vigorous stirring for 6 h at room temperature. The obtained purple precipitate was collected by centrifugation, washed with water and methanol several times and finally dried under vacuum at 60 °C overnight.</span>
        </span></div>
        <div>
          
          <span id="sect340"><span class="c_heading_indent">2.1.2 Preparation of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC. </span>
          <span>In a typical synthesis, the as-prepared purple ZIF-67 was carbonized under the protection of nitrogen at 550 °C for 2 h with a heating rate of 5 °C min<small><sup>−1</sup></small> to acquire the black Co@NC products. The sulfidation process was proceeded through a facile solvothermal process. First, 0.1 g obtained Co@NC products and 0.34 g thioacetamide (TAA, Sinopharm Chemical) were dispersed into a mixture solution of 25 mL DI water and 25 mL ethylene glycol under ultrasonication for 30 min. Then, the mixture was transferred into an 80 mL Teflon-lined stainless steel autoclave and sealed at 180 °C for 24 h. After cooling to room temperature naturally, the sample was collected by centrifugation, washed with DI water and ethanol several times, and finally dried under vacuum at 60 °C overnight.</span>
        </span></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect347"><span class="b_heading">2.2 Materials characterization</span></h3>
        <span>The X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns of the products were characterized using an X-ray diffractometer (XRD, Rigaku, Rint-2000) with Cu-Kα radiation at 0.154 nm. The Raman spectrum was recorded using a Raman spectrometer (DXR, Thermo-Fisher Scientific). Composition measurements were conducted using an X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy analyzer (XPS) (VG Multi Lab 2000 system). The specific surface area and pore size distributions of the obtained product were measured by a Micromeritics ASAP 2460 instrument, while the morphology and crystal lattice of the sample were observed by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) (JSM 6400) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) (JEOL). The content of the carbon in the Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC was investigated in air atmosphere using a thermal analysis instrument (NETZSCH STA449F3) with a heating rate of 5 °C min<small><sup>−1</sup></small> from room temperature to 700 °C.</span>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect354"><span class="b_heading">2.3 Electrochemical measurements</span></h3>
        <span>Electrochemical experiments were performed by utilizing CR2016 coin cells. The electrodes were fabricated by mixing 70 wt% Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC (active materials), 15 wt% conductive carbon, and 15 wt% carboxymethyl cellulose (CMC) binder in deionized water to form a homogenous slurry. Then, the resultant slurry was coated on a copper foil and dried at 80 °C under vacuum overnight. The CR2016 coin cells were assembled in an argon-filled glove box using metallic sodium as the counter electrode and a polypropylene film (Celgard 2400) as the separator. The electrolyte was made by dissolving 1 M NaClO<small><sub>4</sub></small> in propylene carbonate (PC) with the addition of 5 vol% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC). Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were performed in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na) on an Arbin battery cycler. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements were conducted on a Multi Autolab M204 electrochemical station in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) experiments were performed on a Solartron 1470 Multistat system under frequencies ranging from 0.01 Hz to 100 kHz. All electrochemical tests were performed at room temperature.</span>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect363"><span class="a_heading">3. Results and discussion</span></h2>
      
        
        <h3 id="sect366"><span class="b_heading">3.1 Structure and compositions characterization</span></h3>
        <span>The crystallographic structures of the ZIF-67 and Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC were investigated by X-ray powder diffraction. The XRD pattern of ZIF-67, displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1a</a>, confirms its high crystallinity due to the sharp diffraction peaks. For Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC, the XRD analysis pattern depicted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1b</a> suggests that the diffraction peaks are well-matched with the standard XRD pattern of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> (PDF no. 47-1738), and no other phases of cobalt sulfide appeared, manifesting the high purity of the product. Furthermore, the diffraction peaks of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC at around 26.7°, 31.5°, 38.2°, 50.3° and 55.1° are assigned to the (220), (311), (400), (511) and (440) crystal planes of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit40"><sup><span class="sup_ref">40–42</span></sup></a> The Raman spectrum is presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1c</a>, in which the disordered D band and graphitic G band can be detected at around 1342.75 and 1594.81 cm<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. The intensity ratio of D and G bands (<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>D</sub></small>/<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>G</sub></small>) is about 0.95, verifying that the nitrogen-doped carbon is also composed of amorphous and graphitic carbon.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig1"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr05652h-f1.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 1 </b> <span id="fig1"><span class="graphic_title">(a–b) XRD patterns of ZIF-67 and Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC. (c) Raman spectrum of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC. (d) N<small><sub>2</sub></small> adsorption–desorption isotherm and pore size distribution of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC (inset).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Moreover, the specific surface areas and pore-size distribution of the product were analyzed through nitrogen adsorption and desorption measurements. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig1">Fig. 1d</a>, the BET specific surface area of the as-prepared Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC is 176.67 m<small><sup>2</sup></small> g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and the pore size of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC is mainly centered at 3.62 nm, which reasonably demonstrates the mesoporous microstructure.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">In <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>, the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) result of the final product Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC under air atmosphere is presented, in which three stages of weight changes are observed. The weight loss of approximately 3.06% from 20 °C to 200 °C (Stage I) is attributed to the loss of absorbed water, and another two weight changes between 200 °C and 700 °C (Stage II and III) are owing to the conversion of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> into CoSO<small><sub>4</sub></small> (confirmed by XRD in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2a</a>) and the combustion of carbon during the burning process in air, respectively. According to the TGA results, the calculated Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> content is about 56% based on the following equation:</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn1"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-t1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr05652h-t1.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-t1.gif"></a></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(1)</td></tr></tbody></table><p></p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig2"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f2_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr05652h-f2.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f2.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 2 </b> <span id="fig2"><span class="graphic_title">(a) TGA curves of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC and XRD patterns of the combustion product of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC (inset). (b–f) XPS survey and Co 2p, S 2p, C 1s and N 1s spectra of the Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC sample.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">The XPS survey of the sample is shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2b</a>, which indicates the presence of Co, S, C, N and O. As displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2c</a>, the Co 2p spectrum can be resolved into six peaks. The peaks located at 779.4 and 782.01 eV are assigned to Co 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small>, while the peaks at 794.04 and 797.25 eV are attributed to Co 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>. The first couple of peaks at 779.40 and 794.04 eV verify the presence of Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small>, and the second couple of peaks at 782.01 and 797.25 eV confirm the presence of Co<small><sup>3+</sup></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit43"><sup><span class="sup_ref">43</span></sup></a> Moreover, the two broad peaks at 787.03 and 802.61 eV are well-ascribed to the satellite peaks of Co 2p.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> Accompanied with the Co 2p spectrum, the S 2p peaks (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2d</a>) at 162.74 (S 2p<small><sub>3/2</sub></small>) and 163.84 eV (S 2p<small><sub>1/2</sub></small>) further illustrate the existence of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> The C 1s spectrum (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2e</a>) shows four peaks at binding energy of 284.74, 285.96, 287.01 and 288.84 eV, assigned to the carbon atoms in C–C, C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">N, C–N and O–C<img class="charmap" src="https://www.rsc.org/images/entities/char_e001.gif" alt="[double bond, length as m-dash]">O groups, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit44"><sup><span class="sup_ref">44</span></sup></a> Additionally, as exhibited in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig2">Fig. 2f</a>, the three peaks located at around 398.23, 399.80 and 401.89 eV are effectively assigned to pyridinic, pyrrolic and graphitic N, respectively.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit45"><sup><span class="sup_ref">45</span></sup></a></p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect446"><span class="b_heading">3.2. Morphology characterization</span></h3>
        <span>The morphologies of the obtained products were investigated by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) at different magnifications. As displayed in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3a–c</a>, the ZIF-67 precursors are composed of dodecahedron structures with relatively smooth surfaces and typical particle size of about 200 nm. After continuous carbonization and sulfuration, SEM images (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3d–f</a>) of the obtained Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC show that the basic outline of the ZIF-67 is maintained, but the particle size shrinks to about 160 nm and the surfaces become slightly rough. According to <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig3">Fig. 3e</a>, the elemental mappings manifest the uniform distribution of C, N, S, Co elements in Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC. Moreover, the TEM images (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4a–c</a>) of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC further illustrate the existence of polyhedral morphology and enhance the reliability of the SEM results.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig3"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f3_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr05652h-f3.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f3.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 3 </b> <span id="fig3"><span class="graphic_title">(a–c) SEM images of ZIF-67. (d–f) SEM images of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC. (g) Typical SEM image of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC and the corresponding elemental mapping images of carbon (red), nitrogen (green), sulfur (blue) and cobalt (purple), respectively.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig4"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f4_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr05652h-f4.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f4.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 4 </b> <span id="fig4"><span class="graphic_title">(a–c) TEM images, (d) HRTEM image, and (e) SEAD pattern of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, some black points belonging to the nanocrystals can be observed clearly. Based on the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (<a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4d</a>), it is observed that Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC nanoparticles are coated by amorphous and graphitic carbon due to the carbonization of organic chains, and some visible nanocrystals with diameter of about 5 nm are embedded around the carbon, demonstrating the evident lattice spacing of about 0.28 nm, which can be indexed to the (311) lattice plane of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit42"><sup><span class="sup_ref">42,44</span></sup></a> The lattice spacing of 0.34 nm is observed for graphitic carbon. Furthermore, the selected area electron diffraction (SAED), presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig4">Fig. 4e</a>, adequately illustrates the crystallinity of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> in the obtained Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC hybrid.</p>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect482"><span class="b_heading">3.3 Formation mechanism of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC</span></h3>
        <span>As illustrated in <a title="Select to navigate to scheme" href="#imgsch1">Scheme 1</a>, the MOFs-derived Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC was obtained through successive carbonization and sulfuration processes. First, the ZIF-67 precursors were acquired by a precipitation reaction with cobalt nitrate hexahydrate and 2-methylimidazole, which are the source of cobalt ions and the organic ligands, respectively. Co<small><sup>2+</sup></small> species were linked by 2-methylimidazole by strong chemical bonds in the formation of ZIF-67 and then, the precursor was formed. The as-prepared ZIF-67 was then carbonized at 550 °C for 2 h.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a> During the carbonization process under nitrogen atmosphere, the organic ligands were carbonized to N-doped carbon and then, the cobalt ions were reduced to metallic Co particles by the obtained carbon species. Finally, Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC was prepared using thioacetamide as the sulfur source through a solvothermal sulfuration reaction at 180 °C, while the final sample retained the polyhedral morphology of the ZIF-67 precursors.</span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgsch1"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-s1_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr05652h-s1.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-s1.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Scheme 1 </b> <span id="sch1"><span class="graphic_title">Illustration of the formation of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC: (I) carbonization of ZIF-67 under a nitrogen atmosphere, and (II) synthesis of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC <span class="italic">via</span> solvothermal sulfuration.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
      
      
        
        <h3 id="sect502"><span class="b_heading">3.4. Electrochemical properties</span></h3>
        <span>The electrochemical properties of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC were characterized using a series of coin cells. Cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurements of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC were performed under the scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> within the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na/Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>) to investigate the characteristic Na-storage mechanism. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5a</a>, the cathodic peak at 1.02 V was observed in the first cycle, which was ascribed to the reaction of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> insertion, and this cathodic peak shifts to 1.44 V in the subsequent cycles.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30,44</span></sup></a> The insertion reaction between Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> and Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC can be described in the following equation (<a title="" href="#eqn2">eqn (2)</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> In addition, the cathodic peak at 0.76 V can be indexed to the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase and the conversion reaction of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> with Na. However, this peak is divided into two peaks at 0.44 V and 0.85 V in the next four cycles.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a> The conversion reaction of the Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC is illustrated as shown below (<a title="" href="#eqn3">eqn (3)</a>).<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit30"><sup><span class="sup_ref">30</span></sup></a><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn2"><span id="eqn2">Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> + <span class="italic">x</span>Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + <span class="italic">x</span>e<small><sup>−</sup></small> → Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(2)</td></tr></tbody></table><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn3"><span id="eqn3">Na<small><sub><span class="italic">x</span></sub></small>Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> + (8 − <span class="italic">x</span>) Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> + (8 − <span class="italic">x</span>) e<small><sup>−</sup></small> → 4Na<small><sub>2</sub></small>S + 3Co</span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(3)</td></tr></tbody></table></span>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig5"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f5_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr05652h-f5.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f5.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 5 </b> <span id="fig5"><span class="graphic_title">(a) CV curves of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC electrode in a range of 0.01–3.0 V (<span class="italic">vs</span>. Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (b) CV curves at various scan rates from 0.1–1.0 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in a range of 0.01–3.0 V. (c) The fitting line of log(sweep rate, mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small>) <span class="italic">vs.</span> log(<span class="italic">I</span><small><sub>peak</sub></small>, A). (d) The plots of <span class="italic">i</span>/<span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small><span class="italic">vs. v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> at 0.5, 0.75, and 1.0 V of the SIBs using the Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC as anodes. (e) Charge contributions from capacitance and intercalated charge at the voltages of 0.5, 0.75, and 1.0 V at 0.5 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> for Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC. (f) Charge contributions from capacitance and intercalated charge under different scan rates at 0.5 V for Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC.</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">For the anodic process, the peaks at 1.84 V and 2.08 V can be assigned to the reactions of Na<small><sup>+</sup></small> extraction and the conversion reaction of Na with Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>, respectively. During the subsequent cycles, the locations of the two anodic peaks are essentially unchanged, except the peak becomes sharper, but notably, a small peak appears at about 1.97 V due to the division of the anodic peak at 2.08 V, which is well-matched with the cathodic process.</p>
        <p class="otherpara">To further explore the Na-storage behavior of the acquired material, CV measurements under various scan rates from 0.1 to 1.0 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> were collected. As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5b</a>, the CV curves at different scan rates display similar outlines. Furthermore, the intensity of the oxidation and electrochemical charge carrier transfer was analyzed by a functional relationship between the peak current (<span class="italic">i</span>) and scan rate (<span class="italic">v</span>) as shown below (<a title="" href="#eqn4">eqn (4)</a>):</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn4"><span id="eqn4"><span class="italic">i</span> = <span class="italic">av</span><small><sup><span class="italic">b</span></sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(4)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">a</span> and <span class="italic">b</span> are adjustable values. When the <span class="italic">b</span>-value is close to 0.5, the storage capacity is primarily controlled by the diffusion-controlled process, whereas when the <span class="italic">b</span>-value approaches 1.0, the storage capacity mainly originates from the surface-induced capacitive process.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit47"><sup><span class="sup_ref">47,48</span></sup></a> According to the fitted line of log(<span class="italic">v</span>) <span class="italic">vs.</span> log(<span class="italic">i</span>) curve depicted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5c</a>, the <span class="italic">b</span>-value is calculated to be 0.838, which indicates that the capacities originate from both the diffusion-controlled behavior and surface-capacitance behavior.<a title="Select to navigate to references" href="#cit46"><sup><span class="sup_ref">46</span></sup></a><p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, in order to quantify the source of storage capacity, the current (<span class="italic">i</span>) under a certain potential (<span class="italic">V</span>) can be divided into two parts (<a title="" href="#eqn5">eqn (5)</a>):</p><table><tbody><tr><td class="leftEqn">&nbsp;</td></tr><tr><td class="eqn"><span id="eqn5"><span id="eqn5"><span class="italic">i</span>(<span class="italic">V</span>) = <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span> + <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small></span></span></td><td class="rightEqn">(5)</td></tr></tbody></table>where <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small><span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> relate to surface capacitive charge and diffusion-controlled charge, respectively. As presented in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5d</a>, the values of <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> are obtained by plotting and fitting the <span class="italic">i</span>/<span class="italic">v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small><span class="italic">vs. v</span><small><sup>1/2</sup></small> lines, where <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>1</sub></small> and <span class="italic">k</span><small><sub>2</sub></small> correspond to the slope and intercept, respectively. The storage behavior of the product under different voltages (0.5, 1.0, and 1.5 V) at the same scan rate of 0.1 mV s<small><sup>−1</sup></small> are described in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5e</a>, in which the capacity from capacitive charge increased gradually with the increase in voltage, illustrating that the surface-induced capacitive process mainly occurs at a high voltage and the diffusion-controlled process can be accomplished at a relatively lower voltage. Simultaneously, <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig5">Fig. 5f</a> depicts the storage behavior of the material at various scan rates at the voltage of 0.5 V, in which the capacitive contributions gradually increased from 35.8% to 63.8%, indicating that most of the capacity of the material stems from the surface-capacitance behavior at high current density.<p></p>
        <p class="otherpara">
          <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6a</a> exhibits the galvanostatic charge and discharge profiles of the as-prepared material at a current density of 100 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na) for different cycles. The sample shows three discharge plateaus at 1.44 V, 0.85 V and 0.44 V during the discharging process, and two charge plateaus are observed at 1.84 V and 2.08 V in the discharging process. All the reaction plateaus are in good agreement with the CV curves, except the first cycling process, which is probably due to the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI). Furthermore, during the first cycle, the discharge and charge capacities of the electrode are 748.9 and 531.5 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively, with a coulombic efficiency of 70.97%. The relatively low initial coulombic efficiency is attributed to the irreversible formation of the SEI occurring on the surface of the Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC.<a title="Select to navigate to reference" href="#cit49"><sup><span class="sup_ref">49–54</span></sup></a> As shown in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6b</a>, the cycling performance of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC electrode was tested at a current density of 100 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> in the voltage range of 0.01–3.0 V (<span class="italic">vs.</span> Na<small><sup>+</sup></small>/Na) for 100 cycles, in which a capacity of 420.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> and a stable coulombic efficiency of about 99% are obtained after 100 cycles. <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6c</a> presents the rate performances of the as-prepared samples for SIBs at different currents. The anode displays reversible capacities of 491.2, 442.2, 386.7, 324.7, 284.0, 191.8, and 151.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> at current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.4, 0.8, 1.0, 1.2 and 1.4 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. When the current density returns to 0.1 A g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, a stable specific capacity of 403.8 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> is obtained. Moreover, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was employed to analyze the electrochemical impedance in the sodium/Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC cells. As depicted in <a title="Select to navigate to figure" href="#imgfig6">Fig. 6d</a>, the charge transfer resistance of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC of fresh cells is 5287 Ω. However, the charge transfer resistances decrease to 103.8, 93.85 and 81.92 Ω after 5, 15 and 20 cycles, respectively, indicating that the charge transfer resistance tends to be more stable after five cycles due to the wettability of the electrode.</p>
        <br><div class="image_table"><table><tbody><tr><td colspan="3" class="imgHolder" id="imgfig6"><a href="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f6_hi-res.gif" title="Select to open image in new window" onclick="open(this.href, &quot;_blank&quot;, &quot;toolbar=1,scrollbars=yes,resizable=1&quot;); return false;"><img alt="image file: c8nr05652h-f6.tif" src="/image/article/2018/NR/c8nr05652h/c8nr05652h-f6.gif"></a></td></tr><tr><td class="pushTitleRight"> </td><td class="image_title"><b>Fig. 6 </b> <span id="fig6"><span class="graphic_title">(a) Galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC electrodes at current density of 100 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (b) Cycle performance of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC electrodes at current density of 100 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>. (c) Rate performance of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC electrodes at different current densities. (d) Nyquist plots of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC under different cycles, and the corresponding equivalent circuit model (inset).</span></span></td><td class="pushTitleLeft"> </td></tr></tbody></table></div>
        <p class="otherpara">Furthermore, the comparison of electrochemical properties between Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC and other transition metal sulfide anodes for sodium-ion batteries reported in previous literature is provided in <a title="Select to navigate to table" href="#tab1">Table 1</a>. The superior sodium-ion storage performance of Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC can be attributed to the nitrogen-doped carbon coating, the Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> ultrafine nanocrystal and the unique porous structure derived from ZIF-67. First, the nitrogen-doped carbon coating can effectively enhance the electrical conductivity, relieve the volume expansion and obtain excellent cyclability. Second, the Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> ultrafine nanocrystal can reinforce the mass transfer rate, resulting in relatively good rate performances. Third, the porous structure and the large specific area are beneficial for the electrolyte to permeate into the materials, finally leading to high surface-capacitance contribution and high specific capacity.</p>
        
<div class="table_caption"><b>Table 1</b> <span id="tab1">The comparison of electrochemical properties between Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC and other transition metal sulfides anodes for sodium ion batteries</span>
		</div>

          <hr class="hrule"><br>
      
    
    
      
      <h2 id="sect802"><span class="a_heading">4. Conclusion</span></h2>
      <span>In summary, ZIF-derived Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@nitrogen-doped carbon nanohybrid materials have been prepared in this study by successive carbonization and sulfuration processes. The obtained Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small>@NC hybrid contains ultrafine Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> nanocrystals and inherits the mesoporous microstructure with high surface area from the ZIF-67 precursors, which could efficiently reduce the drastic volume change, enhance the mass transfer rate and finally endow the product with high specific capacity, excellent cycling stability and good rate performances. At the current density of 100 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, a capacity of 420.9 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> with high coulombic efficiency is acquired after 100 cycles for SIBs. Moreover, the reversible specific capacities of 386.7, 284.0, and 151.2 mA h g<small><sup>−1</sup></small> can be obtained at 400, 1000, and 1400 mA g<small><sup>−1</sup></small>, respectively. The ZIF-67 derived N-rich carbon-coated Co<small><sub>3</sub></small>S<small><sub>4</sub></small> ultrafine nanocrystal shows excellent electrochemical performance, indicating that the MOF-derived transition metal sulfides can be promising electrode materials for energy-storage devices.</span>
